Seeing Allah in the Afterlife
Sanusi affirms that believers will see Allah in the afterlife with physical eyes but without modality or limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿari doctrine of the beatific vision: that believers will see Allah in Paradise with their physical eyes, but without modality, direction, or limitation.

Core Idea
Believers will see Allah in the afterlife — truly, but without 'how'.

The poem now turns to one of the most important matters of the afterlife: the vision of Allah by the believers. Sanusi affirms that this vision will be a real sight with the eyes, yet completely free from the limitations of physical vision in this world. Let's examine his precise wording.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the key affirmations and restrictions.
The passage is only two lines, but packed with meaning.
2
Understand 'ru'ya bi-l-abṣār'
This phrase means a sensory vision with the eyes, not a metaphorical or intellectual sight. The Ashʿaris affirm this literally against the Muʿtazila.
3
Grasp the two restrictions
Bilā kayf (without modality) means we do not ask 'how' it happens. Lā inḥiṣār (without limitation) means Allah is not confined to a place, direction, or finite form.
These restrictions safeguard tanzih (transcendence).
4
Consider the term 'jā'iz'
The vision is possible (jā'iz) because it is attached to a revealed promise. It is not rationally necessary, but it is established through scripture (samʿiyyāt).
This counters the Muʿtazila claim that vision is rationally impossible.
5
Reflect on the second line
The second line mentions that this vision is for the believers in the afterlife, and possibly for the chosen one (the Prophet) in this world, as in the Miʿrāj.
Some commentators interpret 'al-mukhtār' as the Prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهُ أَنْ يُنْظَرَ بالأَبْصَارِ لكِنْ بِلَا كَيْفَ ولا انْحِصَارِ . لِلْمُؤْمِنِينَ إِذْ بِجائِز عُلقَتْ هَذا ولِلْمُخْتَارِ دُنْيَا ثَبتت .
And from it is that He is seen by (physical) eyes, but without 'how' and without limitation. For the believers, since it is attached to a permissible (matter), this (vision) and for the chosen one (?) it is established in the world?
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage discusses the beatific vision (ru’yat Allāh) in the afterlife. It affirms that Allah will be seen by the eyes (abṣār) of the believers in Paradise, but without modality (kayf) and without limitation (inḥiṣār). This is the Ashʿari position against the Muʿtazila who denied the possibility of seeing Allah. The second line is somewhat corrupt but likely states that this vision is established for the believers in the afterlife, and perhaps for the chosen (al-mukhtār) in this world (as in the case of Prophet Muhammad on the night of Miʿrāj) or that it is a matter of jā’iz (possible) that has been revealed.

Key Terms
الرؤية (al-ru'ya)
بالأبصار (bi-l-abṣār)
بلا كيف (bilā kayf)
انحصار (inḥiṣār)
جائز (jā'iz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That seeing Allah implies direction or corporeality
Correction: The correct view is that it is a true vision without modality or limitation, as per the poem: 'bi-lā kayf wa-lā inḥiṣār'.

Checkpoint
According to the poem, how will believers see Allah in the afterlife?
A) With physical eyes but without 'how' or limitation.
B) With the heart only.
C) In a specific direction.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Believers will see Allah in Paradise with real physical sight, but totally free from the limitations and modality of created vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that you will see Allah with your own eyes in Paradise, with no veil and no limitation, what in your life would have to change right now to make that vision possible?
?
The Prophet (saw) was granted a vision of Allah in this world. What does that tell you about his station? Are you living in a way that honors that station and seeks to follow him?
?
The lesson says the vision is seen 'without modality.' How does accepting 'howlessness' challenge your desire to control or fully understand everything? Are you willing to submit to what Allah has revealed without demanding to know the 'how'?

Guardian Angels and Self-Accounting
Two verses on guardian angels, recording angels, and self-accounting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic beliefs about guardian and recording angels and the importance of self-accounting.

Core Idea
Every person is accompanied by angels who record deeds and must hold themselves accountable.

The poem begins by affirming that every person has appointed angels who guard and record their deeds without negligence. This establishes a foundation of divine oversight. The following verse then calls the believer to take heed by actively accounting for their own soul and reducing worldly hopes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic verses
Read verses 85-86 in Arabic carefully. Notice the terms 'ḥāfiẓūn' and 'kātibūn' for the angels, and the command 'ḥāsibi al-nafs'.
These verses set the tone for personal responsibility.
2
Understand the function of the angels
The first verse states that there are guardians (ḥāfiẓūn) who protect, and writers (kātibūn) who record deeds. They are chosen and never neglect.
This emphasizes constant divine vigilance.
3
Reflect on self-accounting
The second verse commands 'fa-ḥāsibi al-nafs' (so account yourself) and 'qallili al-āmāl' (reduce hope). This is a practical directive to evaluate one's actions and detach from worldly illusions.
Self-accounting is a key spiritual practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٨٥ - بِكُلِّ عَبْدٍ حافظونَ وُكُلُوا وكاتبونَ خِيرَةٌ لَنْ يُهْمِلُوا ٨٦ - فَحَاسِبِ النَّفْسَ وقَلّل الاملا ٨٧ - مِنْ أَمْرِهِ شَيْئًا فَعَلْ وَلَوْ ذَهِلْ الأنينَ فى المرض كما نُقِلْ ٨٨ - فرُبَّ مَنْ جَدَّ لِأَمْرٍ وَصَلا ٨٩ - وَوَاجِبُ إيمانُنَا بالموتِ ويَقْبِضُ الرُّوْحَ رَسُولُ الموتِ ٩٠ - ومَيِّتُ بِعُمْرِهِ مَنْ يُقْتَلُ وغير هذا باطل لا يُقْبَلُ ٩١ - وفي فَنَا النَّفْسِ لَدَى النَّفْخِ اخْتُلِفْ واستظْهَرَ السُّبكيْ بَقَاهَا اللَّذْ عُرِفْ ٩٢ - عَجْبُ الذَّنَبْ كالرُّوحِ لكنْ صَحَّحَا المُزَنيُّ لِلْبِلَى وَوَضَّحَا
85. For every slave there are guardians entrusted, and writers, chosen ones, who will not neglect. 86. So account yourself and reduce hope. 87. Do any of His command even if forgetful, the groaning in illness as reported. 88. For perhaps one who strives for a matter attains it. 89. And obligatory is our faith in death, and the angel of death takes the soul. 90. And the killed is [one who dies by] his lifespan; other than this is false, not accepted. 91. And regarding the annihilation of the soul at the blowing (of the trumpet), there is disagreement, and al-Subki preferred its survival as known. 92. The coccyx bone is like the soul, but al-Muzani affirmed decay for it and clarified.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
These verses cover various eschatological topics. Verse 85 states that every person has guardian angels (ḥāfiẓūn) and recording angels (kātibūn) who are entrusted and chosen, never neglecting their duties. Verse 86 commands self-accounting (muḥāsaba) and reducing vain hopes. Verse 87 advises not to neglect any of God's commands even if forgetful, and mentions groaning in illness as reported (perhaps a hadith). Verse 88 notes that striving may lead to attainment. Verse 89 affirms obligatory belief in death and that the Angel of Death (Malak al-Mawt, identified as 'Azrael) takes the soul. Verse 90 states that the person killed dies at his appointed lifespan; any other view is false. Verse 91 mentions a disagreement about the annihilation of the soul at the trumpet blast (the first Nafkh), with al-Subki preferring the known view that the soul survives. Verse 92 compares the 'ajb al-dhanab (coccyx bone) to the soul, but al-Muzani affirmed its decay (corruption) and clarified the matter.

Key Terms
الحفظة
الكتبة
محاسبة النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What do the 'kātibūn' angels do according to verse 85?
A) They protect from harm
B) They write down deeds
C) They punish sinners
D) They deliver revelations

Key Takeaway
Angels are constantly guarding and recording, so we must consciously account for our own actions and reduce worldly hopes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if angels are recording every word you speak and every thought you entertain? What one habit will you change today because of this awareness?
?
What worldly hope are you holding onto that stops you from preparing for the Hereafter? Will you take a concrete step to reduce it by tomorrow?
?
When was the last time you sincerely accounted for your deeds – not just regretted, but planned to improve? What specific action will you take tonight to start a daily self-accounting practice?

Action, Striving, and Death
Four verses on action, striving, death, and the angel of death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of fulfilling God's commands even forgetfully, the value of striving, and the obligatory belief in death and the angel of death.

Core Idea
Strive in obedience and accept death as an appointed moment, for effort leads to attainment.

These verses guide the believer in practical effort and correct belief about death. They encourage doing any of God's commands even if forgetful, and highlight that striving may lead to success. Then they affirm the obligatory belief in death and the angel of death, emphasizing that every killed person dies exactly by his appointed lifespan.

Guided Path
1
Read verses 87-90
Read the Arabic lines. Notice the encouragement in verse 87-88 and the doctrinal statement in 89-90.
Verses 87-88 focus on action; 89-90 on death.
2
Understand the call to action
Verse 87 says do any of God's command even if forgetful, and mentions groaning in illness as reported. Verse 88 says perhaps one who strives attains his goal.
This teaches persistence and hope in effort.
3
Grasp the creed about death
Verses 89-90 state: obligatory faith in death, the angel of death takes the soul, and the killed person dies by his appointed lifespan; any other view is false.
This affirms predestination and the angel's role.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٨٥ - بِكُلِّ عَبْدٍ حافظونَ وُكُلُوا وكاتبونَ خِيرَةٌ لَنْ يُهْمِلُوا ٨٦ - فَحَاسِبِ النَّفْسَ وقَلّل الاملا ٨٧ - مِنْ أَمْرِهِ شَيْئًا فَعَلْ وَلَوْ ذَهِلْ الأنينَ فى المرض كما نُقِلْ ٨٨ - فرُبَّ مَنْ جَدَّ لِأَمْرٍ وَصَلا ٨٩ - وَوَاجِبُ إيمانُنَا بالموتِ ويَقْبِضُ الرُّوْحَ رَسُولُ الموتِ ٩٠ - ومَيِّتُ بِعُمْرِهِ مَنْ يُقْتَلُ وغير هذا باطل لا يُقْبَلُ ٩١ - وفي فَنَا النَّفْسِ لَدَى النَّفْخِ اخْتُلِفْ واستظْهَرَ السُّبكيْ بَقَاهَا اللَّذْ عُرِفْ ٩٢ - عَجْبُ الذَّنَبْ كالرُّوحِ لكنْ صَحَّحَا المُزَنيُّ لِلْبِلَى وَوَضَّحَا
85. For every slave there are guardians entrusted, and writers, chosen ones, who will not neglect. 86. So account yourself and reduce hope. 87. Commentary
These verses cover various eschatological topics. Verse 85 states that every person has guardian angels (ḥāfiẓūn) and recording angels (kātibūn) who are entrusted and chosen, never neglecting their duties. Verse 86 commands self-accounting (muḥāsaba) and reducing vain hopes. Key Terms
الموت
رسول الموت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a person who is killed dies before their appointed time.
Correction: Verse 90 clarifies that the killed person dies by his lifespan; all deaths occur at the predetermined moment.

Checkpoint
According to verse 90, what is the ruling on the view that a killed person dies before his appointed lifespan?
A) It is acceptable
B) It is false and not accepted
C) It is a matter of opinion
D) It depends on the intention

Key Takeaway
Belief in death and the angel of death is obligatory, and every soul dies at its appointed time; meanwhile, believers are to strive in obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself complaining during illness or hardship, do you recall the command to act 'even if forgetful' (ولو ذهل)? What specific step can you take today to obey Allah despite your current difficulty?
?
The verse says 'رب من جد لأمر وصل' — many who strive attain. Is there an act of worship or obedience you have been neglecting because you doubt you will succeed? Will you commit to striving in it starting today, trusting that effort itself is rewarded?
?
Do you truly live as if your death is appointed to the very moment by Allah's angel, or do you fear accidents and illnesses as if they could take you before your time? How would your daily priorities shift if you internalized that your lifespan is fixed?

The Soul and the Coccyx Bone
Two verses on the soul's annihilation and the coccyx bone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic discussion on whether the soul perishes at the trumpet blast and the role of the coccyx bone as the basis for resurrection.

Core Idea
The soul's survival after the trumpet blast is a point of scholarly disagreement, but the coccyx bone remains as the seed for resurrection.

The final two verses address eschatological details: disagreement among scholars about the annihilation of the soul at the first trumpet blast, with al-Subki preferring its survival, and the nature of the coccyx bone which al-Muzani argued decays, unlike the soul. These show the precision of theological debates on resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Read verses 91-92
Read the Arabic lines. Note the terms 'fanā al-nafs' (annihilation of the soul) and 'ajb al-dhanab' (coccyx bone).
These are technical issues in eschatology.
2
Understand the disagreement on the soul
Verse 91 mentions a disagreement (ikhtalaf) about the annihilation of the soul at the trumpet blast. Al-Subki preferred its survival.
This shows there is no consensus on this detail.
3
Learn about the coccyx bone
Verse 92 compares the coccyx bone to the soul, but al-Muzani affirmed its decay (balā) and clarified the matter.
The coccyx is said to be the origin from which the body is resurrected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٨٥ - بِكُلِّ عَبْدٍ حافظونَ وُكُلُوا وكاتبونَ خِيرَةٌ لَنْ يُهْمِلُوا ٨٦ - فَحَاسِبِ النَّفْسَ وقَلّل الاملا ٨٧ - مِنْ أَمْرِهِ شَيْئًا فَعَلْ وَلَوْ ذَهِلْ الأنينَ فى المرض كما نُقِلْ ٨٨ - فرُبَّ مَنْ جَدَّ لِأَمْرٍ وَصَلا ٨٩ - وَوَاجِبُ إيمانُنَا بالموتِ ويَقْبِضُ الرُّوْحَ رَسُولُ الموتِ ٩٠ - ومَيِّتُ بِعُمْرِهِ مَنْ يُقْتَلُ وغير هذا باطل لا يُقْبَلُ ٩١ - وفي فَنَا النَّفْسِ لَدَى النَّفْخِ اخْتُلِفْ واستظْهَرَ السُّبكيْ بَقَاهَا اللَّذْ عُرِفْ ٩٢ - عَجْبُ الذَّنَبْ كالرُّوحِ لكنْ صَحَّحَا المُزَنيُّ لِلْبِلَى وَوَضَّحَا

Verse 86 commands self-accounting (muḥāsaba) and reducing vain hopes. Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the coccyx bone never decays and remains incorruptible like the soul.
Correction: Al-Muzani affirmed its decay (balā) – it also perishes, but it is the origin from which the body is reconstituted.

Checkpoint
What did al-Subki prefer regarding the soul at the trumpet blast according to verse 91?
A) Its annihilation
B) Its survival
C) Its transformation
D) Its punishment

Key Takeaway
The soul likely survives the trumpet blast, while the coccyx bone decays but serves as the seed for bodily resurrection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the soul survives the trumpet blast, what are you doing now to prepare your soul for that moment? Are you feeding it with faith or neglecting it?
?
The coccyx bone decays but is the seed for your resurrection. What in your life—your struggles, losses, or failures—is 'decaying' that Allah might use to raise you to something greater? How does that change your view of hardship?
?
Scholars disagreed about the soul's annihilation, yet both sides sought truth sincerely. When you face uncertainty in matters of the unseen, does your heart rest on the core promises of Allah, or do you let doubt shake your foundation? What would it take for you to trust Him completely?

Specification of 'Everything Perishes'
Verse 93 on the specification of 'Everything perishes'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars have specified the general Quranic statement 'Everything is perishing' and why it is important to follow their specification.

Core Idea
Everything perishes save His Face, but scholars specified its application.

This verse from al-Jawhara addresses a key Quranic verse (Q28:88) that everything perishes except Allah's Face. The poet notes that scholars have specified (khaṣṣaṣū) the generality of this verse, meaning they identified what is exempted. It is a reminder that apparent general statements in revelation may have specific applications known to the scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic verse once and its English translation. Notice the verb 'khaṣṣaṣū' (they specified).
2
Understand specification (takhṣīṣ)
Reflect on the concept of specifying a general statement. Why do scholars do this? How does it apply to this verse?
3
Connect to broader lesson
Ask yourself why the poet begins this section with this verse. What does it teach about approaching scripture?
This verse sets the tone for the subsequent eschatological topics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٩٣ - وكُلَّ شَيءٍ هالك قد خَصَّصوا عُمُومَهُ فَاطْلُبْ لِمَا قَدْ لخَصُوا ٩٤ - ولا نَخْض في الرُّوحِ إِذْ ما وَرَدَا نَضٌ عَنِ الشَّارِعِ لَكِنْ وُجِدَا ٩٥ - لمالك هي صُورَةٌ كالجسد فَحَسْبُك النَّصّ بهذا السَّنَدِ ٩٦ - والعقل كالرُّوحِ وَلكنْ قَرَّرُوا فيهِ خِلافًا فَانْظُرَنْ مَا فَسَّرُوا ٩٧ - سُؤَالُنا ثُمَّ عَذَابُ القَبرِ نَعِيمُهُ واجِبْ كَبَعْثِ الحَشْرِ ٩٨ - وَقُلْ يُعَادُ الجِسْمُ بالتَّحْقِيقِ عَنْ عَدَمٍ وَقِيلَ عَن تَفْرِيقِ محضين لكنْ ذا الخلافُ خُصَّا بِالأَنْبِيَا وَمَنْ عَلَيْهِمْ نُصَّا ٩٩ - وفي إعادةِ العَرَض قولانِ ورجـحـتْ إعادةُ الأَعْيَانِ
93. And everything is perishing; they specified its generality, so seek what they have specified. 94. And we do not delve into the spirit, since no text from the Lawgiver has come, but it has been found. 95. For Malik, it is a form like the body, so sufficient for you is the text with this chain. 96. And the intellect is like the spirit, but they have established disagreement regarding it, so look at what they explained. 97. Our questioning, then the torment of the grave, its bliss, is obligatory like the resurrection of the gathering. 98. And say: the body is returned, in reality, from non-existence; and it is said from mere disintegration. But this disagreement is specifically for prophets and those designated by text. 99. And regarding the return of accidents, there are two opinions, and the return of substances was preponderated.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
These verses cover more eschatological and theological issues. Verse 93 refers to the Quranic verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face' (Q28:88), which scholars have specified (khaṣṣaṣū) to exclude certain things; one should seek what they specified. Verse 94 advises not to delve into the nature of the spirit (rūḥ) because no clear text from the Lawgiver (al-Shāri') has come about its detailed nature, although some information exists. Verse 95 reports Imam Malik's view that the spirit is a form (ṣūra) like the body, and that the transmitted text is sufficient. Verse 96 compares the intellect ('aql) to the spirit, noting that scholars have established disagreement about it, so one should look at their explanations. Verse 97 affirms the obligation to believe in the questioning in the grave (su'āl), the torment ('adhāb) and bliss (na'īm) of the grave, which is as obligatory as believing in the resurrection (ba'th) and gathering (ḥashr). Verse 98 concerns the return of the body: it is truly returned from non-existence ('adam), though some say from mere disintegration (tafrīq). This disagreement is specifically for prophets and those designated by text (maybe martyrs?). Verse 99 discusses the return of accidents (a'rāḍ): there are two opinions, and the preponderant view is the return of substances (a'yān).

Key Terms
الهالك
العموم
الخصوص
التخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Everything perishes' means literally everything, including Paradise and Hell, will cease to exist.
Correction: Scholars specify that the verse means everything other than Allah, and some creations like Paradise and Hell are not subject to perishing in the same way.

Checkpoint
What does the poet instruct the student to do regarding the specification of the verse?
A) Accept it without inquiry
B) Seek what they have specified
C) Reject the specification
D) Speculate on its meaning

Key Takeaway
General statements in revelation may have specific applications determined by scholars; one must seek that specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Spirit and Intellect
Verses 94-96 on the spirit and intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the true nature of the spirit is not known through revelation, and that Malik considered it a form, while the intellect is subject to scholarly disagreement.

Core Idea
The spirit is known only by transmitted text, not rational inquiry.

These verses address two subtle matters: the spirit (rūḥ) and the intellect (ʿaql). The poet advises not to delve into the spirit because no explicit text from the Lawgiver explains its essence, though some reports exist. He then reports Imam Malik's view that the spirit is a form like the body. Regarding the intellect, scholars have differed, so one should look to their explanations.

Guided Path
1
Read verses 94-96

The lack of a clear text from the Lawgiver is key.
3
Note the disagreement on the intellect
The intellect is compared to the spirit, but scholars have established disagreement. What does this imply about how we should approach such matters?

The Benefit of Supplication
Sanusi states that supplication benefits as promised in the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine that supplication (du'a) is beneficial and that its basis is a promise from the Quran.

Core Idea
Supplication benefits because the Quran promises it.

This lesson presents a concise but important article of faith: that calling upon Allah is not in vain. The poet affirms the efficacy of supplication, grounding it directly in a Quranic promise. This is a matter of sam'iyyat (scriptural matters).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic line
Read the Arabic line carefully: وَعِنْدَنا أَنَّ الدُّعاءَ يَنْفَعُ كما مِنَ القُرْآنِ وَعْدًا يُسْمَعُ.
Notice the rhythm and the key terms.
2
Understand the doctrinal claim
The line asserts that supplication (du'a) benefits (yanfa'u). This is a core Sunni belief that prayer has real effects.
Contrast with views that claim supplication is useless.
3
Identify the proof
The proof is 'a promise heard from the Quran' (wa'dan yusma'u). The Quran contains promises that supplication is accepted.
This shows the doctrine is based on revelation, not reason alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَعِنْدَنا أَنَّ الدُّعاءَ يَنْفَعُ كما مِنَ القُرْآنِ وَعْدًا يُسْمَعُ
And with us [we believe] that supplication benefits, as a promise heard from the Quran.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage affirms the efficacy of supplication (du'a). It is a statement of Sunni doctrine that prayer and petition to God are beneficial—they can bring about good or ward off harm. The proof is that the Quran contains promises that supplication is heard and answered. The phrase 'as a promise heard from the Quran' indicates that this is not a rational invention but a revealed truth.

Guardian and Recording Angels
Sanusi affirms the existence of guardian and recording angels for every person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every person has two types of angels: guardians who protect and recording angels who write down deeds, and that these angels are chosen and never neglect their duty.

Core Idea
Every person has appointed angels who guard and record, and they never fail.

Sanusi affirms the existence of guardian and recording angels for every person. This belief is grounded in the Qur'an and Sunnah. In this lesson, we will examine the precise attributes of these angels as stated by Sanusi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style – each word carries significant meaning.
2
Identify the two types of angels
The passage mentions 'guardians' (حافظون) and 'writers' (كاتبون). Understand their functions: protection and recording.
3
Understand the attributes
The angels are described as 'entrusted' (وُكُلُوا), 'chosen' (خِيرَةٌ), and 'who will not neglect' (لَنْ يُهْمِلُوا). Consider what each attribute implies about their nature and reliability.
These attributes emphasize divine selection and perfect performance.
4
Reflect on personal accountability
Knowing that we are constantly watched and recorded by reliable angels should inspire mindfulness and responsibility in our actions.
This belief is meant to encourage righteousness, not paranoia.

Source Text

The Resurrection of the Body: From Nothing or Dispersion
Sanusi clarifies two views on bodily resurrection and their scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two views on bodily resurrection in Islamic theology and that the dispute is restricted to prophets.

Core Idea
The resurrection of the body is from nothing, but a dispute applies only to prophets.

Sanusi clarifies the modality of bodily resurrection in this concise verse. While the verified position is that bodies are recreated from absolute non-existence, a minority view suggests reassembly from scattered particles. However, this disagreement is not universal—it is limited to prophets and certain individuals specifically mentioned in scripture.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic line
Read the Arabic verse once to get the rhythm. Then read the literal translation. Notice the two parts: the statement of the two views and the restriction.
The verse is a single couplet; it's short but dense.
2
Identify the two views
The first part presents the verified view: resurrection from non-existence (عَنْ عَدَمٍ). Then it mentions an alternative view: from a pure dispersion (عَن تَفْرِيقِ مَحْضَين). Understand what each entails.
'Pure dispersion' means the body is reassembled from its scattered atoms.
3
Understand the restriction
The second part says 'but this dispute is specific to prophets and those for whom it is stipulated.' This means the disagreement about resurrection from dispersion only applies to prophets and certain saints, not to all humans.
For ordinary people, the only position is resurrection from non-existence.
4
Consider the implication
Reflect on why Sanusi makes this distinction. It shows the care in Ash'ari theology to safeguard the generality of bodily resurrection while acknowledging textual nuances.
This lesson guards against the misconception that the dispute applies to all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقُلْ يُعَادُ الجِسْمُ بالتَّحْقِيقِ عَنْ عَدَمٍ وَقِيلَ عَن تَفْرِيقِ مَحْضَين لكنْ ذا الخلافُ خُصَّا بِالأَنْبِيَا وَمَنْ عَلَيْهِمْ نُصَّا
And say: The body is returned, according to verification, from non-existence, and it was said from a pure dispersion. But this dispute is specific to prophets and those for whom it is stipulated.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage addresses the modality of bodily resurrection. The verified (tahqiq) view is that the body is recreated from absolute non-existence (adam). Another view says it is reassembled from its scattered particles (tafriq mahdayn). The poet notes that this disagreement applies only to the bodies of prophets and those specifically mentioned in texts, implying that for others the resurrection is from non-existence without dispute.

Key Terms
الجِسْمُ
عَنْ عَدَمٍ
عَن تَفْرِيقِ
مَحْضَين
الخلافُ خُصَّا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute about resurrection from dispersion applies to all humans.
Correction: The poet explicitly restricts the dispute to prophets and those for whom it is stipulated, so for others resurrection is from non-existence.

Checkpoint
Who does the poet restrict the dispute about resurrection from dispersal to?
A) All believers
B) Prophets and those stipulated
C) Martyrs
D) Scholars

Key Takeaway
The resurrection of the body is from non-existence, but a limited dispute about reassembly from dispersion applies only to prophets and stipulated individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You've just learned that your own resurrection will be from absolute non-existence, not a reassembly of your current body. Does this truth change how you view your physical body now? Will you live today with the awareness that this body will be completely annihilated and remade by God?
?
The poet restricts a dispute about resurrection mode to prophets only. Are you comfortable submitting to a theological detail that you may not fully understand? Where in your faith do you need to trust God's wisdom over your need for full explanation?
?
If the resurrection of prophets is a matter of scholarly disagreement, does that affect your reliance on their intercession or your certainty in their status? What does this teach you about the limits of human knowledge regarding the unseen?

Divine Justice and Mercy: Recompense and Forgiveness
Sanusi explains the reckoning: evil deeds repaid alike, good deeds multiplied, and minor sins forgiven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principles of divine recompense for evil and good deeds, and the means by which minor sins are forgiven according to Sunni theology.

Core Idea
Allah multiplies good deeds, repays evil equally, and forgives minor sins through avoiding major sins and performing wudu.

This passage from Sanusi's Jawhara al-Tawhid succinctly summarizes key beliefs about divine justice and mercy. It teaches that while evil deeds are repaid precisely, good deeds are multiplied as a grace. Furthermore, minor sins are expiated by avoiding major sins and through the act of wudu, a practice rooted in prophetic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm. Then read the literal English translation. Notice that the passage is a concise statement of several related doctrines.
2
Identify the two categories of deeds
The passage first mentions evil deeds (al-sayyi'at) and good deeds (al-hasanat). Observe how each is treated: evil deeds are recompensed 'by the like' (bil-mithl), while good deeds are multiplied by grace (al-fadl).
This establishes a foundational contrast between justice and grace.
3
Understand the conditions for forgiveness
Next, the passage introduces forgiveness of minor sins (sagha'ir) through avoiding major sins (kaba'ir). Reflect on how this reflects the Sunni doctrine that minor sins are forgiven when major sins are shunned.
This is a key belief often misunderstood; it does not mean one can commit minor sins freely.
4
Recognize the role of wudu
Finally, the passage states that wudu (ablution) expiates (yukaffir). Consider that this refers to the expiation of minor sins as taught in hadith, not that wudu forgives major sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالسَّيِّنَاتُ عِنْدَهُ بالمثل والحسناتُ ضُوعِفَتْ بالفَضْل وَبِاجْتِنَابِ لِلْكبَائِرُ تُغْفَرُ صَغَائِرٌ وَجَا الوُضُو يُكَفِّرُ
So evil deeds with Him are [repaid] by the like, and good deeds are multiplied by grace. And by avoiding major sins, minor sins are forgiven, and ablution (wudu) expiates.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage describes divine justice and mercy. Evil deeds are recompensed with an equivalent punishment, while good deeds are multiplied in reward as a grace (fadl). Furthermore, if one avoids major sins (kaba'ir), minor sins (sagha'ir) are forgiven—a standard Sunni belief. Additionally, performing wudu (ablution) expiates minor sins, as reported in hadith.

Key Terms
السَّيِّنَاتُ
بالمثل
الحسناتُ
ضُوعِفَتْ
الْكبَائِرُ
صَغَائِرٌ
الوُضُو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think every sin must be punished, leaving no room for forgiveness of minor sins.
Correction: The passage clarifies that minor sins are forgiven through avoiding major sins and through acts like wudu, showing God's mercy alongside justice.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what expiates minor sins?
A) Fasting
B) Avoiding major sins
C) Both avoiding major sins and wudu
D) Charity

Key Takeaway
Divine justice ensures equivalence for evil, grace multiplies good, and mercy forgives minor sins through avoiding major sins and performing wudu.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah multiplies your good deeds, or do you often feel your efforts are insignificant? How would your daily actions change if you lived with certainty in His boundless grace?
?
When you commit a minor sin, do you casually assume it will be forgiven because you avoid major sins, or does it drive you to increase your wudu and turn back to Allah in repentance?
?
Are you relying more on your own avoidance of major sins or on the expiation through wudu? Which means of forgiveness are you neglecting, and what does that reveal about the state of your heart?

The Reality of the Last Day and the Terror
Sanusi affirms the Last Day and the terror, then prays for mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Last Day and the terror of the standing before Allah are true and that the poet prays for mercy.

Core Idea
The Last Day and its terror are certain; we beseech Allah for ease.

Sanusi begins this passage by affirming a foundational truth of the Muslim creed: the reality of the Last Day and the intense terror of standing before Allah. He then turns to supplication, asking the Merciful to lighten that terror and grant aid. This lesson focuses on that affirmation and prayer.

Guided Path
1
Read the affirmation
Read the first part of the passage: 'واليوم الآخِرُ ثُمَّ هَوْلُ الموقِفِ حَقٌّ'. Note that it is a direct assertion of truth.
The word 'حق' indicates certainty.
2
Notice the supplication
The poet then prays: 'فَخَفِّفْ يا رحيم واسْعِفِ'. Reflect on the shift from doctrine to personal plea.
This shows that belief leads to action and reliance on Allah.
3
Connect to your own belief
Consider how affirming the Last Day impacts your life and motivates mindfulness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واليوم الآخِرُ ثُمَّ هَوْلُ الموقِفِ حَقٌّ فَخَفِّفْ يا رحيم واسْعِفِ وواجبٌ أَخْذُ العِبَادِ الصُّحُفَا كما مِنَ القُرآن نَصَّا عُرِفَا وَمِثْلُ هَذا الوَزْنُ والميزانُ فَتُوْزَنُ الكُتُبُ أَوِ الأَعْيانُ كذا الصِّراطُ فالعِبَادُ مُخْتلِف مُرورُهُمْ فسالمٌ وَمُنْتَلِف
And the Last Day, then the terror of the standing is true; so lighten [it], O Merciful, and assist. And obligatory is the taking of the records by the servants, as known from the Quran text. And like this is the weighing and the balance, so the books or the entities are weighed. Likewise the bridge, so the servants' crossing differs, so some are safe and some are damaged.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage affirms several eschatological realities. First, the Last Day and the terror of the standing (mawqif) before God are true. The poet then prays for God's mercy. Second, the giving of records (suhuf) to each person is obligatory belief, based on the Quran. Third, the weighing (wazn) with a balance (mizan) will take place, whether weighing the books or the deeds themselves (a'yan). Fourth, the bridge (sirat) is real, and people's crossing will vary: some will pass safely, others will fall or be harmed. All these are articles of faith.

Key Terms
اليوم الآخِرُ
هَوْلُ الموقِفِ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The poet affirms it as true (حق), indicating a literal, real terror.

Checkpoint

B) To lighten it and assist
C) To ignore it
D) To remove it completely

Key Takeaway
The Last Day and its terror are real; we should implore Allah for mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Giving of Records and the Weighing
Sanusi states obligation of records and weighing, citing Quran.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Records will be given and deeds weighed – this is from the Quran.

After affirming the Last Day, Sanusi moves to specific events of that Day. Both the books (records) and the deeds themselves (a'yan) are weighed.
3

The Bridge (Sirat) and the Crossing
Sanusi affirms the bridge and differing outcomes for people.

Learning Objective

The sirat is real; people cross differently – some to safety, some to harm.

The final eschatological reality mentioned in this passage is the bridge (sirat). Sanusi affirms its existence and describes the varying outcomes of people crossing it. Some will pass safely, while others will suffer damage. This lesson focuses on this belief and its implications.

Paradise, Hell, and the Basin of the Prophet
Sanusi affirms the reality of Paradise, Hell, the Basin, and intercession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligatory belief in the created reality of Paradise and Hell, the eternity of both abodes, and the obligation to believe in the Prophet's Basin and the condition of drinking from it.

Core Idea
Paradise and Hell are real, created, and eternal; belief in the Prophet's Basin is mandatory.

This passage from Sanusi's Jawharat al-Tawhid affirms several fundamental beliefs of Ahl al-Sunnah regarding the afterlife. It begins with the reality and createdness of Hell and Paradise, then addresses the eternity of both abodes, and concludes with the obligation of belief in the Prophet's Basin (Hawd) and who will drink from it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without breaking it down. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the strong language: 'So do not incline to a denier who is insane.'
2
Identify the three affirmations
The passage contains three distinct creedal points: (1) Paradise and Hell are real and created, (2) they are eternal abodes, (3) belief in the Prophet's Basin is obligatory with conditions on who drinks.
3
Understand the condition for drinking
The poet says those who fulfilled their covenant drink, while transgressors are driven away. Reflect on what 'covenant' might mean—generally, obedience to Allah.
This refutes any claim that all Muslims automatically drink without qualification.
4
Connect to samiyyat
These beliefs are part of samiyyat (revealed matters) that must be accepted as they came. Denying them is denial of what the Prophet brought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنَّارُ حقٌّ أُوجدَتْ كالجنَّةِ فَلَا تَمِلْ لِجَاحِدٍ ذي جِنَّةِ دارا خُلُودٍ للسعيدِ والشَّقِي مُعَذِّبٌ مُنَعَمٌ مَهمَا بَقِي إيماننا بحوض خَيرِ الرُّسُلِ حَتْمٌ كَمَا قَدْ جَاءَنَا فِي النَّقْلِ يَنَالُ شُرْبًا منه أقوامٌ وَفَوا بعهدهِم وَقُلْ يُدَادُ مَنْ طَغَوا
And the Fire is true, created like the Garden. So do not incline to a denier who is insane. Two abodes of eternity for the blessed and the wretched, a tormentor and a benefactor as long as [they] remain. Our belief in the Basin of the best of Messengers is an obligation, as has come to us in transmission. Some people obtain drink from it who fulfilled their covenant, and say: he who transgressed is driven away.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage affirms several beliefs. First, Hellfire and Paradise are both true and created (ujidat). Denying them is like insanity. They are eternal abodes for the blessed and the wretched. Second, belief in the Basin (Hawd) of Prophet Muhammad is obligatory (hatm). Some people will drink from it—those who kept their covenant—while those who transgressed will be driven away (yudadu). The Basin is a specific body of water granted to the Prophet in the hereafter.

Key Terms
النَّارُ
الجنَّةِ
خُلُودٍ
حوض
يُدَادُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's Basin is a metaphor or not real.
Correction: Sanusi makes belief in the Basin an obligation (ḥatm) and describes it as a real source from which some drink and from which others are driven away, based on transmitted reports.

Checkpoint

D) Non-Muslims

Key Takeaway
Belief in the reality of Paradise and Hell, their eternity, and the Prophet's Basin are obligatory doctrines transmitted in revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you live as if the Fire is real and created now, or do you act as if it is a distant abstraction? What would it mean for you to truly internalize that eternal bliss or torment has already been prepared?
?

?
Sanusi calls the denier of Hell 'insane'. Do you ever find yourself intellectually denying or minimizing the severity of sin because you forget the reality of Hell? How can you remind yourself daily of this truth?

Intercession of the Prophet and the Righteous
Sanusi declares the obligation of intercession and its broader scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why belief in intercession (shafa'ah) is obligatory and that it is not limited to the Prophet.

Core Idea
Intercession is a promised gift, not to be denied.

The Prophet's intercession is a cornerstone of Sunni hope. This lesson clarifies that affirming it is obligatory, and that other righteous figures also intercede as transmitted in authentic reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on intercession
Carefully read the Arabic lines where Sanusi states the obligation of intercession and mentions other intercessors.
Notice the command 'do not prevent' indicating denial is forbidden.
2
Identify who intercedes
The passage mentions 'other from the chosen righteous.' Note that intercession is not exclusive to the Prophet.
This is based on reports (أخبار) which include hadith.
3
Reflect on the ruling
Consider why denying intercession is prohibited. What does this teach about hope in Allah's mercy?
Intercession is a form of honor and mercy from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ وَاجِبٌ شَفَاعَةُ المُشفّعِ مُحَمَّدٍ مُقَدَّمًا لا تَمْنَعِ وَغيرُهُ من مُرْتَضَى الأَخْيَارِ يَشْفَعُ كما قَدْ جاءَ في الأخبارِ إِذْ جَائِرٌ غُفْرانُ غير الكُفْرِ فلا نُكَفِّرْ مؤمنًا بالوِزْرِ وَمَنْ يَمُتْ ولم يَتُبْ مِنْ ذَنْبِهِ فأمرُهُ مُفَوَّضٌ لِرَبِّهِ
And obligatory is the intercession of the intercessor Muhammad—do not deny it—and others from the chosen righteous intercede as has come in reports. For forgiveness other than disbelief is permissible, so we do not excommunicate a believer for sin. And whoever dies without repenting of his sin, his affair is entrusted to his Lord.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage covers intercession and the fate of sinful believers. First, belief in the intercession (shafa'ah) of Muhammad is obligatory, and it must not be denied. Other righteous people (e.g., prophets, saints) also intercede as reported. Second, it states that forgiveness (ghufran) of anything other than disbelief is permissible (ja'iz) for God; therefore, we do not excommunicate (tukaffir) a believer for committing a sin (wizr). Finally, the person who dies without repenting of a sin is left to God's will—He may forgive or punish. This is the Sunni position on grave sinners.

Key Terms
شَفَاعَةُ
المُشفّعِ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that only the Prophet Muhammad intercedes and that no one else can.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that others from the righteous also intercede, based on reports.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling on the intercession of Muhammad?
A) It is recommended but not obligatory
B) It is obligatory to believe in
C) It is forbidden to believe in
D) It depends on one's deeds

Key Takeaway
Belief in the intercession of the Prophet and other righteous is an obligatory part of Sunni creed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly doubted that the Prophet’s intercession applies to you—perhaps because of your sins—and if so, what does this lesson challenge in your heart? What will you actively do today to strengthen your hope in his intercession?
?
When you hear that even the righteous can intercede, do you feel a sense of comfort or a call to strive to be among them? How will you change your daily actions to increase your chances of being counted among the 'chosen righteous'?
?
This lesson warns 'do not deny it'—is there any part of your belief system that subtly denies or minimizes Allah’s mercy or the means He has given? What would it look like to fully embrace this obligation in your prayer life and thoughts about the Hereafter?

Ruling on Sinful Believers
Sanusi articulates the Sunni position on sin and excommunication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a believer is not excommunicated for sin and that the fate of unrepentant sinners is left to Allah's will.

Core Idea
Sin does not break faith; final judgment belongs to Allah.

The Sunni position balances mercy and justice: grave sins do not expel one from faith, and unrepentant sinners are entrusted to God's infinite wisdom. This lesson draws the lines between excommunication and hope.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on forgiveness
Read the Arabic lines that discuss permissible forgiveness and the prohibition of excommunication.
The word 'ja'ir' here means permissible, not obligatory.
2
Identify what is permissible for God
Note that forgiveness (ghufran) is permissible for anything except disbelief (kufr).
This implies that major sins are forgivable if Allah wills.
3
Understand the rule on excommunication
The passage states 'we do not excommunicate a believer for sin'—this is a key Sunni principle against Kharijites.
The word 'nukaffir' refers to declaring someone a disbeliever.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ وَاجِبٌ شَفَاعَةُ المُشفّعِ مُحَمَّدٍ مُقَدَّمًا لا تَمْنَعِ وَغيرُهُ من مُرْتَضَى الأَخْيَارِ يَشْفَعُ كما قَدْ جاءَ في الأخبارِ إِذْ جَائِرٌ غُفْرانُ غير الكُفْرِ فلا نُكَفِّرْ مؤمنًا بالوِزْرِ وَمَنْ يَمُتْ ولم يَتُبْ مِنْ ذَنْبِهِ فأمرُهُ مُفَوَّضٌ لِرَبِّهِ
And obligatory is the intercession of the intercessor Muhammad—do not deny it—and others from the chosen righteous intercede as has come in reports. For forgiveness other than disbelief is permissible, so we do not excommunicate a believer for sin. And whoever dies without repenting of his sin, his affair is entrusted to his Lord.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage covers intercession and the fate of sinful believers. First, belief in the intercession (shafa'ah) of Muhammad is obligatory, and it must not be denied. Other righteous people (e.g., prophets, saints) also intercede as reported. Second, it states that forgiveness (ghufran) of anything other than disbelief is permissible (ja'iz) for God; therefore, we do not excommunicate (tukaffir) a believer for committing a sin (wizr). Finally, the person who dies without repenting of a sin is left to God's will—He may forgive or punish. This is the Sunni position on grave sinners.

Key Terms
جَائِرٌ غُفْرانُ
نُكَفِّرْ
الوِزْرِ
مُفَوَّضٌ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some groups (like Kharijites) hold that committing a major sin makes one a disbeliever.
Correction: The Sunni position states that a believer remains a believer even if he sins; excommunication is not allowed.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what happens to a believer who dies without repenting of a sin?
A) He is eternally damned
B) He is automatically forgiven
C) His affair is entrusted to Allah
D) He becomes a disbeliever

Key Takeaway
A believer is never excommunicated for sin, and the final judgment of unrepentant sinners belongs solely to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

On Reckoning, Deeds, and Minor Sins
Three verses on reckoning, deeds, minor sins, and wudu's expiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three key tenets of Sunni belief: the certainty of the reckoning, the proportional recompense of deeds, and the forgiveness of minor sins through avoiding major sins and wudu.

Core Idea
The reckoning is true; good deeds multiply; minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins.

This passage from Jawharat al-Tawhid presents three foundational beliefs about the afterlife and divine justice. It affirms the certainty of the reckoning, explains the proportional recompense of evil deeds and multiplied reward for good deeds, and clarifies that minor sins are forgiven through avoidance of major sins, with wudu also serving as expiation. Let's examine each verse with its meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the verses and translation
Read the Arabic verses (100-102) and their literal translation once to get the overall meaning.
Notice the concise poetic form.
2
Identify the three statements
Verse 100 speaks about the reckoning, verse 101 about recompense, and verse 102 about minor sins and wudu.
3
Understand the doctrinal significance
The reckoning is true without doubt; evil deeds are punished equally, good deeds multiplied; minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins.
This reflects divine justice and grace.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some think wudu alone forgives all sins, but it only expiates in context of avoiding major sins.
Review the misconception in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a set of three verses from the poem Jawharat al-Tawhid. Verse 100 states that there are two opinions regarding the time (of reckoning) but that the reckoning itself is true without doubt. Verse 101 explains that evil deeds are recompensed with an equivalent punishment, while good deeds are multiplied by divine grace. Verse 102 teaches that minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins, and that wudu (ablution) has an expiatory effect on sins.

The Last Day and Its Realities
Five verses on Last Day, books, balance, path, and cosmic entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key eschatological realities affirmed by the author: the Last Day, the giving of books, the weighing of deeds, the bridge, and the cosmic entities (Throne, Chair, Pen, Tablet).

Core Idea
The Last Day's realities are literal and known from the Quran.

This passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid lists several essential beliefs about the afterlife and cosmic realities. The author affirms each as true and obligatory, drawing directly from Quranic texts. As you read, note how each element is introduced with a clear claim of truth or obligation.

Guided Path
1
Verse 103: The Last Day and Terror
Read the first line affirming the Last Day and the terror of the standing-place, and note the supplication for mercy.
This sets the tone for eschatological events.
2
Verse 104: Receiving the Books
Understand that taking the books of deeds is obligatory, based on Quranic texts.
The Quran mentions the book being given in the right or left hand.
3
Verse 105: The Balance
Notice that the weighing involves either the books or the deeds themselves, as the verse states.
This clarifies a point of theological discussion.
4
Verse 106: The Path (Sirat)
Read about the straight path over hell, where people cross differently—some safe, others fall.
This is a literal bridge in Islamic eschatology.
5
Verse 107: Cosmic Entities
List the Throne, Chair, Pen, and Tablet, and note that all are 'wise' (full of wisdom).
These are created entities, not allegorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٠٣ - واليوم الآخِرُ ثُمَّ هَوْلُ الموقِفِ حَقٌّ فَخَفِّفْ يا رحيم واسْعِفِ ٥٢٧ ١٠٤ - وواجبٌ أَخْذُ العِبَادِ الصُّحُفَا كما مِنَ القُرآن نَصَّا عُرِفَا ٥٢٩ ١٠٥ - وَمِثْلُ هَذا الوَزْنُ والميزانُ فَتُوْزَنُ الكُتُبُ أَوِ الأَعْيانُ ١٠٦ - كذا الصِّراطُ فالعِبَادُ مُخْتلِف ،مُرورُهُمْ ، فسالمٌ وَمُنْتَلِف ٥٣٧ ١٠٧ - والعَرْسُ والكُرْسِيُّ ثُمَّ القَلَمُ والكاتبونَ اللّوحُ كُلّ حِكَمُ ٥٤٠
103 - And the Last Day, then the terror of the standing-place, is true; so lighten (it), O Merciful, and assist. 104 - And it is obligatory for the servants to take the books, as known from the Qur'an text. 105 - And similar to this is the weight and the balance, so the books or the entities are weighed. 106 - Likewise the path, so the servants differ in their crossing: one safe and one falling. 107 - And the Throne and the Chair, then the Pen, and the writers, the Tablet—all are wise.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a series of verses listing eschatological realities. Verse 103 affirms the Last Day and the terror of the standing-place (al-mawqif) as true, and includes a supplication for mercy. Verse 104 declares the obligatory nature of receiving one's book (of deeds) as known from the Qur'an. Verse 105 mentions the weighing (al-wazn) and the balance (al-mizan), and clarifies that either the books or the deeds themselves are weighed. Verse 106 describes al-sirat (the bridge over hell) and that people cross it differently—some safe, others slipping. Verse 107 lists other exalted realities: the Throne (al-'arsh), the Chair (al-kursi), the Pen (al-qalam), the writers, and the Tablet (al-lawh), all of which are described as 'wise' (hukam, i.e., full of wisdom).

Misconception: Some may deny the literal nature of these eschatological objects, considering them symbolic.
Correction: The passage affirms them as true in their literal sense, as known from the Quran.

C) Either the books or the deeds.

The Reality of Paradise, Hell, and the Prophet's Basin
Five verses affirming creation and eternity of Paradise, Hell, and the Prophet's basin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Paradise and Hell are created realities, that they are eternal abodes for the righteous and wicked respectively, and that belief in the Prophet's basin (al-hawḍ) is obligatory based on transmitted reports.

Core Idea
Paradise and Hell are created, eternal, and the Prophet's basin is real.

In these verses, the poet affirms fundamental eschatological truths: the Fire and Paradise are created and eternal, and the Prophet's basin is a reality that the faithful will drink from. These are matters of faith based on revelation, not reason, and any denial is a form of disbelief. Let us examine each verse carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read verses 108-109
Read the verses about the obligation of faith and the reality of Fire and Paradise being created. Note the warning against denying this.
Verse 108 mentions 'not due to need'—this refers to God's self-sufficiency; faith is obligatory for humans.
2
Understand verse 110
This verse describes Paradise and Hell as two eternal abodes, one of bliss and one of torment, for the happy and the wretched respectively.
The word 'خلود' implies everlastingness.
3
Understand verses 111-112
These verses affirm belief in the Prophet's basin as certain, based on transmitted reports. Some drink from it fulfilling their covenant, transgressors are prevented.
The basin (حوض) is a specific eschatological feature granting water to the faithful.
4
Identify the common misconception
Some argue Paradise and Hell are not yet created. The passage directly counters this by stating the Fire is created (أُوجدَت) like Paradise.
This misconception arises from certain theological positions; the verse settles it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٠٨ - لَا لِاحْتِيَاجِ وَبِهَا الإِيمَانُ يجبْ عَلَيكَ أَيُّهَا الإِنْسَانُ ٥٤٢ ١٠٩ - والنَّارُ حقٌّ أُوجدَتْ كالجنَّةِ فَلَا تَمِلْ لِجَاحِدٍ ذي جِنَّةِ ٥٤٣ ١١٠ - دارا خُلُودٍ للسعيدِ والشَّقِي مُعَذِّبٌ مُنَعَمٌ مَهمَا بَقِي ٥٤٦ ١١١ - إيماننا بحوض خَيرِ الرُّسُلِ حَتْمٌ كَمَا قَدْ جَاءَنَا فِي النَّقْلِ ٥٤٨ ١١٢ - يَنَالُ شُرْبًا منه أقوامٌ وَفَوا بعهدهِم وَقُلْ يُدَادُ مَنْ طَغَوا
108 - Not due to need, and by it faith is obligatory upon you, O human. 109 - And the Fire is true, created like Paradise, so do not incline to a denier who is mad. 110 - Two abodes of eternity: for the happy and the wretched, one tormented, one blissful, as long as they remain. 111 - Our faith in the basin of the best of messengers is certain, as it has come to us in transmission. 112 - Some people attain drinking from it, fulfilling their covenant, and say: those who transgress are prevented.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage covers eschatological realities. Verse 108 seems to state that belief in these matters is obligatory, not due to any need of God. Verse 109 affirms that Hellfire is a created reality, just like Paradise, and warns against rejecting deniers who are 'mad' (dhī jinnah, i.e., possessed or insane). Verse 110 describes both Paradise and Hell as eternal abodes—one of bliss for the happy, one of torment for the wretched. Verses 111–112 discuss the Prophet's basin (al-hawḍ); belief in it is obligatory based on transmitted reports, where some people will drink from it, fulfilling their covenant, while those who transgressed are prevented (yudād).

Punishment of Major Sinners
Sanusi declares the obligation of punishment for some major sinners and the avoidance of eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that believers who commit major sins may be punished but will not remain in Hell eternally.

Core Idea
Major sinners are punished, but eternal punishment is avoided.

This passage addresses a critical question in Islamic theology: what happens to a believer who commits a major sin? The Sanusiyya text clarifies that while punishment may be due for some, eternal damnation is not the fate of such a believer. Let's examine the precise wording to grasp this nuanced position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic line
Read the Arabic line carefully and notice its concise structure.
The line consists of two clear claims separated by 'thumma'.
2
Identify the two claims
The first claim is that punishment is obligatory for some (ba'd) who commit a major sin. The second claim is that eternity (khulud) is avoided.
The word 'some' is significant—it does not say all.
3
Reflect on the meaning
Consider why 'some' and 'avoided' are used. This reflects divine mercy and the possibility of intercession, reserving eternal punishment for disbelievers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَوَاجِبٌ تَعْذِيبُ بَعْضِ ارْتَكَبْ كبيرةً ثُمَّ الخلودُ مُجْتَنَبْ وَصِف شَهِيدَ الحَرْبِ بالحياةِ وَرَزْقِهِ مِنْ مُشْتَهى الجنَّاتِ
And obligatory is punishment of some who commit a major sin, then eternity (in Hell) is avoided. And describe the martyr of war with life and his provision from the desired things of Paradise.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage addresses two separate but related matters. First, the punishment of those who commit major sins (kabira). It states that some will be punished but that eternal punishment (khulud) is avoided, reflecting the Ash'ari position that believers who commit major sins will not remain in Hell forever. Second, it commands to describe the war martyr (shahid al-harb) as being alive and receiving provision from the pleasures of Paradise, in accordance with Quranic verses about martyrs living and being provided for.

Key Terms
كبيرة (major sin)
الخلود (eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that no one who commits a major sin will ever be punished.
Correction: The text specifies punishment for 'some' major sinners, so punishment is not entirely negated; only eternal punishment is avoided.

Checkpoint

A) He will be punished eternally
B) Eternal punishment is avoided
C) He will not be punished at all
D) Only the repentance can save him

Key Takeaway
Believers who commit major sins may be punished temporarily, but they will not remain in Hell forever.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider that the martyr receives provision from 'the desired things of Paradise' immediately upon death. Do you live with a genuine longing for Paradise and its pleasures, or are you satisfied with temporary worldly desires? What is one desire you can sacrifice today to increase your hope for the delights of Paradise?

Hope for Proof and Final Salawat
The poet asks for proof on the Day of Judgment and sends blessings on the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the poet's concluding plea for divine proof on the Day of Questioning and his invocation of blessings upon the Prophet and his followers.

Core Idea
The believer's ultimate hope is divine proof at judgment; our final act is sending peace upon the Prophet of mercy.

The poet concludes his creedal poem not with doctrine alone, but with a heartfelt supplication. He seeks Allah's gift of proof at the moment of questioning—the ultimate test. Then, he seals his work by invoking prayers and peace upon the Prophet Muhammad, whose entire character was mercy, and upon those who follow his path. This lesson explores these closing lines and their spiritual significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the closing supplication
Read the Arabic lines and their translation. Notice the poet's shift from doctrinal argument to personal hope and invocation.
The final word 'امنة' is unclear but may be 'آمين' (amen).
2
Understand the 'questioning'
Consider what 'al-su’al' refers to—likely the questioning in the grave and on the Day of Judgment. Reflect on why the poet asks for proof (حجة) at that moment.
3
Analyze the salawat
Identify the specific groups mentioned: the Prophet, his companions, his family, and followers of his path. Reflect on why the poet includes all these groups in his blessing.
The Prophet's 'habit of mercy' (دأبه المراحم) highlights his compassionate nature.

Meaning of Al and Ruling on Salat
Definition of Al and ruling on salat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'Al' as the pious of the Ummah, the ruling on sending blessings upon them, and a key grammatical issue regarding the conjunction.

Core Idea
The family of the Prophet are the righteous of his community; sending blessings on them has a nuanced ruling.

This lesson delves into a critical aspect of the blessings upon the Prophet: who are his family (Al) and how should we send blessings upon them? The commentator clarifies that the family refers to the pious followers, not just blood relatives. He then discusses the ruling on sending blessings independently upon the family and companions, and addresses a grammatical objection raised by Shaykh al-Islam. This blend of theology, jurisprudence, and Arabic grammar is essential for proper supplication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall structure, then read the literal translation. Note the footnote markers.
The passage includes two footnotes that are integral to the discussion.
2
Understand the definition of 'Al'
Focus on the first part defining 'Al' as the pious of the Ummah. Notice why it is conjoined to 'Prophet' or 'Muhammad'.
3
Explore the ruling on sending blessings
Read footnote (1) carefully. Note the distinction between subordinate and independent salat, and the three opinions leading to the majority view of karaha.
This is a classic example of ikhtilaf (disagreement) resolution.
4
Examine the grammatical issue
Read footnote (2) to understand why Shaykh al-Islam objected to conjoining 'and his family' directly to 'Muhammad' instead of 'Prophet'.
This shows how precise Arabic grammar affects theological formulations.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these details matter: they preserve the proper wording of the salawat and avoid theological errors.
The 'cut-off' blessing is prohibited, so including the family is important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى الآل والصحب والحزب] (وَ) سلامُ الله مع صلاته على (آله) (۱)، وهم أتقياء أتقياء أمته،؛ لتعميم الدعاء، فهو معطوف على نبي، أو محمد (۲)، لمشاركته له في حكمه، وهو الدُّعاء بما ذُكر. (۱) في كلام المصنف هنا الصَّلاة على غير الأنبياء والملائكة تبعاً، وهي جائزة بالإجماع، بل هي مطلوبة؛ لقوله: «اللهم صل على محمد وعلى آل محمد» ولِنَهْيهِ عن الصَّلاة البَتْرَاء، وقد فسَّروها بالصَّلاة التي لم يُذكر فيها الآل، فأمَّا الصَّلاة على الآل والصحب استقلالا فقيل: لا تجوز، بل هي حرام، وقيل: هي مكروهة، وقيل: هي خلاف الأولى، والذي صححه جَمْهَرَةُ العلماء القول بالكراهة. محي. (۲) منع شيخ الإسلام أن يُعطف قوله: (وآله) على قوله: (محمد) وذهب إلى أنَّه فاسد، ووجه ذلك: أنَّ المعطوف في حكم المعطوف عليه، و«محمد» بدل من قوله: «نبي» فلو جعلت قوله (وآله) معطوفا على (محمد) لَزِم أن يكون الآلُ ومَن ذكر معه بدلا أيضا من نبي، وهو لا يجوز. محي.
[Meaning of the family, companions, and party] (And) the peace of Allah along with His blessings upon his family (1) — and they are the pious, the pious of his Ummah — in order to generalize the supplication, so it is conjoined to 'Prophet' or 'Muhammad' (2), due to its sharing with him in the ruling, which is the supplication with what was mentioned. (1) In the author's speech here, sending blessings upon other than prophets and angels is done subordinately, and it is permissible by consensus, indeed it is required, because of his saying: 'O Allah, send blessings upon Muhammad and upon the family of Muhammad,' and because of his prohibition of the 'cut-off' blessing, which they interpreted as the blessing in which the family is not mentioned. As for sending blessings upon the family and companions independently, it is said: it is not permissible, rather it is forbidden; and it is said: it is disliked (makruh); and it is said: it is contrary to what is preferable. And what the majority of scholars affirmed is the opinion of dislike (karaha). (2) Shaykh al-Islam forbade that his saying 'and his family' be conjoined to his saying 'Muhammad', and he held that it is invalid. The reason is that the conjoined item is subject to the ruling of the item to which it is conjoined, and 'Muhammad' is a substitute (badal) for his saying 'Prophet'. So if you made his saying 'and his family' conjoined to 'Muhammad', it would necessitate that the family and those mentioned with him also be a substitute for 'Prophet', and that is not permissible.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of 'Al' (family) and the ruling on sending blessings (salat) upon them. It first defines 'Al' as the pious of the Ummah, indicating that the term is not restricted to blood relatives but includes the righteous followers. The supplication 'peace and blessings upon his family' is conjoined either to the word 'Prophet' or to 'Muhammad' to include them in the prayer. The footnote then discusses the ruling on sending blessings upon non-prophets. It states that doing so subordinately (i.e., when mentioned after the Prophet) is unanimously permissible and even recommended, based on the hadith 'O Allah, send blessings upon Muhammad and the family of Muhammad' and the prohibition of the 'cut-off' blessing that excludes the family. However, sending blessings upon the family and companions independently (i.e., without mentioning the Prophet) is subject to disagreement: some say forbidden, some say disliked (makruh), and some say contrary to preference. The majority opinion is that it is disliked. The second footnote addresses a grammatical issue raised by Shaykh al-Islam: that conjoining 'and his family' to 'Muhammad' is invalid because 'Muhammad' is a substitute for 'Prophet'; conjoining would force the family to also be a substitute, which is not permissible.

Key Terms
آل
صحب
صلاة
كراهة
بدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al refers only to the Prophet's blood relatives.
Correction: The passage defines Al as 'the pious of the Ummah', emphasizing spiritual kinship and righteous followers, not just blood lineage.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of the majority on sending blessings upon the family and companions independently?
A) Permissible
B) Forbidden
C) Disliked (makruh)
D) Mandatory

Key Takeaway
The term 'Al' encompasses the pious of the Ummah, and sending blessings upon them independently is considered disliked by the majority of scholars, while grammatical precision in the formulation of the salawat is maintained by conjoining to 'Prophet' rather than 'Muhammad'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the salawat in your prayer, do you consciously include the family of the Prophet? How does knowing that the majority consider independent salat disliked affect your practice — are you willing to follow that even if it means changing a lifelong habit?
?
The definition of 'Al' as the pious of the Ummah means that your piety determines your connection to the Prophet's family. If you were judged by that standard today, would you consider yourself part of his family? What concrete action can you take this week to increase your piety?
?
Shaykh al-Islam's grammatical argument shows that even a single conjunction can carry deep implications for our worship. Are there phrases in your daily supplications that you say without understanding their meaning or correctness? Will you commit to learning the precise wording of at least one du'a you frequently recite?

The Objection of the Prophet's Father Being in Hell and the Answer
Sanusi presents an objection about the Prophet's father and gives multiple answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the principle that punishment only occurs after revelation by addressing a challenging hadith about the Prophet's father being in Hell, using a hierarchy of textual evidence and contextual interpretations.

Core Idea
Solitary hadiths cannot override definitive Qur’anic verses.

This passage tackles a difficult theological objection: if the Prophet himself said his father is in Hell, how can we maintain that people are not punished until after a messenger is sent? Sanusi provides multiple nuanced answers, showing how to respect the hadith while upholding the Qur’anic principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Read the opening question that cites the hadith where the Prophet says his father is in Hell. Understand the challenge it poses to the principle of no punishment before revelation.
2
Analyze the First Answer
Sanusi’s first response argues that the hadith is solitary (āḥād) and cannot override a definitive Qur’anic verse. Notice the distinction between types of evidence.
3
Examine the Remaining Answers
Follow the subsequent answers: the specific knowledge of Allah, restriction to those who changed religion, and the possibility that they were followers of a previous prophet. Note the critique of the third answer.
4
Synthesize the Lesson
Reflect on how Sanusi maintains the integrity of both the hadith and the Qur’anic principle. Understand the hierarchy of evidence and the role of interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: كيف هذا أخبر بأن جماعة من أهل الفترة من آباء النبي الصحابة في النار ، حيث سئل وهو على المنبر فقيل له أين أبي ؟ فقال: في النار . أجيب: بأن أحاديثهم آحاد لا تعارض القطعي وهو الآية ، وبأنه يجوز أن يكون تعذيب من صح تعذيبه منهم . الأمر يخص به علم الله ورسوله ، وبأن التعذيب مقصور على من غير وبدل من أهل الفترة بما لا يعذر فيه كعبادة الأوثان ، ولكن هذا لا يوافق إطلاق الأئمة ، ولا القول بأنه لا وجوب إلا بالشرع، وبأنه يمكن أن يكون من ثبت تعذيبه من أتباع من بقي شرعه إذ ذاك كعيسى
If you say: How is this? He informed that a group of the people of the interval (ahl al-fatrah) from the fathers of the Prophet, the Companions, are in the Fire, when he was asked while on the pulpit, 'Where is my father?' and he said: 'In the Fire.' The answer: Because their hadiths are isolated (āḥād) and do not contradict the definitive (al-qaṭʿī), which is the verse; and because it is possible that the punishment of those whose punishment is established from them is a matter that Allah and His Messenger know specifically; and because punishment is restricted to those who changed and altered from the people of the interval in a way that is inexcusable, such as worship of idols, but this does not agree with the absolute statements of the imams, nor with the statement that there is no obligation except by revelation; and because it is possible that those whose punishment is established are followers of one whose law remained at that time, like Jesus.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (istifhām) and its answer (jawāb) concerning the status of the Prophet’s father. The objector cites a hadith where the Prophet says his father is in Hell. The answer provides multiple arguments: (1) such hadiths are solitary (āḥād) and cannot override a definitive text (the Qur’anic verse that says We do not punish until We send a messenger). (2) The punishment of specific individuals is known only to Allah and His Messenger. (3) Punishment is only for those who changed religion in an inexcusable way, like idol worship, but this view conflicts with some imams. (4) It is possible that those punished were followers of a previous prophet whose law was still in effect, like Jesus. Thus, the objection is countered without rejecting the hadith outright, but by contextualizing it.

Key Terms
أهل الفترة
آحاد
قطعي
تعذيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith about the Prophet's father being in Hell definitively proves he is in Hell, contradicting the Qur'anic principle of no punishment before revelation.
Correction: The hadith is solitary (āḥād) and cannot override the definitive Qur'anic verse. Additionally, the answers show that the hadith may refer to specific individuals or contexts that do not negate the general principle.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection about the Prophet’s father being in Hell?
A) By denying the hadith completely.
B) By arguing that the hadith is solitary and does not contradict the definitive Qur’anic verse.
C) By claiming the Prophet’s father was not actually his father.
D) By saying the hadith is fabricated.

Key Takeaway
Solitary hadiths that seem to conflict with definitive principles must be interpreted in light of those principles, not used to override them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto a single hadith to support a belief, ignoring a clearer Qur'anic principle? What will you do now when you encounter such a conflict?
?
The objection about the Prophet’s father stirs deep emotions. Are you willing to submit your emotional attachments to the definitive teachings of the Qur'an, even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
The passage suggests that some people of the interval may have had their own prophets (like Jesus). Does this challenge your assumption that everyone without Islam is automatically condemned? How can you trust Allah's perfect justice while leaving such matters to His knowledge?
